	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >Joker (character)</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Fictional character in the DC Universe</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"The Joker" redirects here. For other characters or uses of (The) Joker, see <a href="/wiki/Joker_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Joker (disambiguation)">Joker</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">




</p>

<p>The <b>Joker</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Supervillain" title="Supervillain">supervillain</a> who appears in <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">American comic books</a> published by <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a>. The Joker was created by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Finger" title="Bill Finger">Bill Finger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Kane" title="Bob Kane">Bob Kane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Robinson" title="Jerry Robinson">Jerry Robinson</a> and first appeared in the debut issue of the comic book <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(comic_book)" title="Batman (comic book)">Batman</a></i> on April 25, 1940. Credit for the Joker's creation is disputed; Kane and Robinson claimed responsibility for the Joker's design while acknowledging Finger's writing contribution. Although the Joker was planned to be killed off during his initial appearance, he was spared by editorial intervention, allowing the character to endure as the <a href="/wiki/Archenemy" title="Archenemy">archenemy</a> of the superhero <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a>.
</p><p>In his comic book appearances, the Joker is portrayed as a criminal mastermind. Introduced as a <a href="/wiki/Psychopathy" title="Psychopathy">psychopath</a> with a warped, sadistic sense of humor, the character became a goofy prankster in the late 1950s in response to regulation by the <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code Authority</a>, before returning to his darker roots during the early 1970s. As Batman's nemesis, the Joker has been part of the superhero's defining stories, including the murder of <a href="/wiki/Jason_Todd" title="Jason Todd">Jason Todd</a>—the second <a href="/wiki/Robin_(character)" title="Robin (character)">Robin</a> and Batman's ward—and the paralysis of one of Batman's allies, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Gordon" title="Barbara Gordon">Barbara Gordon</a>. The Joker has had various possible origin stories during his decades of appearances. The most common story involves him falling into a tank of chemical waste that bleaches his skin white and turns his hair green and lips bright red; the resulting disfigurement drives him insane. The antithesis of Batman in personality and appearance, the Joker is considered by critics to be his perfect adversary.
</p><p>The Joker possesses no superhuman abilities, instead using his expertise in chemical engineering to develop poisonous or lethal concoctions and thematic weaponry, including razor-tipped <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">playing cards</a>, deadly <a href="/wiki/Joy_buzzer" title="Joy buzzer">joy buzzers</a>, and acid-spraying lapel flowers. The Joker sometimes works with other <a href="/wiki/Gotham_City" title="Gotham City">Gotham City</a> supervillains, such as the <a href="/wiki/Penguin_(character)" title="Penguin (character)">Penguin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Two-Face" title="Two-Face">Two-Face</a>, and groups like the Injustice Gang and Injustice League, but these relationships often collapse due to the Joker's desire for unbridled chaos. The 1990s introduced a romantic interest for the Joker in his former psychiatrist, <a href="/wiki/Harley_Quinn" title="Harley Quinn">Harley Quinn</a>, who became his villainous sidekick and later escaped an <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">abusive relationship</a> with him. Although his primary obsession is Batman, the Joker has also fought other heroes, including <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wonder_Woman" title="Wonder Woman">Wonder Woman</a>.
</p><p>One of the most iconic characters in popular culture, the Joker has been listed among the greatest comic book villains and fictional characters ever created. The character's popularity has seen him appear on a variety of merchandise, such as clothing and collectible items, inspire real-world structures (such as theme park attractions), and be referenced in a number of media. The Joker has been adapted in live-action, animated, and video game incarnations, including the 1960s <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(TV_series)" title="Batman (TV series)">Batman</a></i> television series played by <a href="/wiki/Cesar_Romero" title="Cesar Romero">Cesar Romero</a> and in films by <a href="/wiki/Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson">Jack Nicholson</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(1989_film)" title="Batman (1989 film)">Batman</a></i> (1989), <a href="/wiki/Heath_Ledger" title="Heath Ledger">Heath Ledger</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">The Dark Knight</a></i> (2008), <a href="/wiki/Jared_Leto" title="Jared Leto">Jared Leto</a> in the <a href="/wiki/DC_Extended_Universe" title="DC Extended Universe">DC Extended Universe</a> (2016–present), and <a href="/wiki/Joaquin_Phoenix" title="Joaquin Phoenix">Joaquin Phoenix</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Joker_(2019_film)" title="Joker (2019 film)">Joker</a></i> (2019); Ledger and Phoenix each earned an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> for their portrayals. <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hamill" title="Mark Hamill">Mark Hamill</a> and others have provided the character's voice ranging from animation to video games.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_and_development">Creation and development</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept">Concept</span></h3>

Veidt's grinning visage inspired the Joker design.</div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bill_Finger" title="Bill Finger">Bill Finger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Kane" title="Bob Kane">Bob Kane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Robinson" title="Jerry Robinson">Jerry Robinson</a> are credited with creating the Joker, but their accounts of the character's conception differ, each providing his own version of events. Finger's, Kane's, and Robinson's versions acknowledge that Finger produced an image of actor <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Veidt" title="Conrad Veidt">Conrad Veidt</a> in character as Gwynplaine (a man with a disfigured face, giving him a perpetual grin) in the 1928 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Laughs_(1928_film)" title="The Man Who Laughs (1928 film)">The Man Who Laughs</a></i> as an inspiration for the Joker's appearance, and Robinson produced a sketch of a <a href="/wiki/Joker_(playing_card)" title="Joker (playing card)">joker playing card</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Creation1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation1-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Robinson claimed that it was his 1940 card sketch that served as the character's concept, and that Finger associated with Veidt's portrayal.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup> Kane hired the 17-year-old Robinson as an assistant in 1939, after he saw Robinson in a white jacket decorated with his own illustrations.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation4_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation4-4">[4]</a></sup> Beginning as a <a href="/wiki/Letterer" title="Letterer">letterer</a> and background inker, Robinson quickly became primary artist for the newly created <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(comic_book)" title="Batman (comic book)">Batman</a></i> comic book series. In a 1975 interview in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_World_of_DC_Comics" title="The Amazing World of DC Comics">The Amazing World of DC Comics</a></i>, Robinson said he wanted a supreme arch-villain who could test Batman, not a typical crime lord or gangster designed to be easily disposed of. He wanted an exotic, enduring character as an ongoing source of conflict for Batman (similar to the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Professor_Moriarty" title="Professor Moriarty">Professor Moriarty</a>), designing a diabolically sinister-but-clownish villain.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation5_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation5-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Origin3_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin3-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETollin19752–3_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETollin19752–3-7">[7]</a></sup> Robinson was intrigued by villains; he believed that some characters are made up of contradictions, leading to the Joker's sense of humor. He said that the name came first, followed by an image of a playing card from a deck he often had at hand: "I wanted somebody visually exciting. I wanted somebody that would make an indelible impression, would be bizarre, would be memorable like the <a href="/wiki/Hunchback_of_Notre_Dame" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunchback of Notre Dame">Hunchback of Notre Dame</a> or any other villains that had unique physical characters."<sup id="cite_ref-Creation8_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation8-8">[8]</a></sup> He told Finger about his concept by telephone, later providing sketches of the character and images of what would become his iconic Joker playing-card design. Finger thought the concept was incomplete, providing the image of Veidt with a ghastly, permanent <a href="/wiki/Risus_sardonicus" title="Risus sardonicus">rictus grin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation5_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation5-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kane countered that Robinson's sketch was produced only after Finger had already shown the Gwynplaine image to Kane, and that it was only used as a card design belonging to the Joker in his early appearances.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation1_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation1-3">[3]</a></sup> Finger said that he was also inspired by an image in <a href="/wiki/Steeplechase_Park" title="Steeplechase Park">Steeplechase Park</a> at <a href="/wiki/Coney_Island" title="Coney Island">Coney Island</a> that resembled a Joker's head, which he sketched and later shared with future editorial director <a href="/wiki/Carmine_Infantino" title="Carmine Infantino">Carmine Infantino</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation2-9">[9]</a></sup> In a 1994 interview with journalist <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lovece" title="Frank Lovece">Frank Lovece</a>, Kane stated his position:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Bill Finger and I created the Joker. Bill was the writer. Jerry Robinson came to me with a playing card of the Joker. That's the way I sum it up. [The Joker] looks like Conrad Veidt – you know, the actor in <i>The Man Who Laughs</i>, [the 1928 movie based on the novel] by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>. ... Bill Finger had a book with a photograph of Conrad Veidt and showed it to me and said, 'Here's the Joker.' Jerry Robinson had absolutely nothing to do with it, but he'll always say he created it till he dies. He brought in a playing card, which we used for a couple of issues for him [the Joker] to use as his playing card.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation101_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation101-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KaneLovece_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KaneLovece-11">[11]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Robinson credited himself, Finger, and Kane for the Joker's creation. He said he created the character as Batman's larger-than-life nemesis when extra stories were quickly needed for <i>Batman</i> #1, and he received credit for the story in a college course:<sup id="cite_ref-Creation102_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation102-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In that first meeting when I showed them that sketch of the Joker, Bill said it reminded him of Conrad Veidt in <i>The Man Who Laughs</i>. That was the first mention of it ... He can be credited and Bob himself, we all played a role in it. The concept was mine. Bill finished that first script from my outline of the persona and what should happen in the first story. He wrote the script of that, so he really was co-creator, and Bob and I did the visuals, so Bob was also.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation103_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation103-13">[13]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Finger provided his own account in 1966:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I got a call from Bob Kane.... He had a new villain. When I arrived he was holding a playing card. Apparently Jerry Robinson or Bob, I don't recall who, looked at the card and they had an idea for a character ... the Joker. Bob made a rough sketch of it. At first it didn't look much like the Joker. It looked more like a clown. But I remembered that Grosset & Dunlap formerly issued very cheap editions of classics by <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas" title="Alexandre Dumas">Alexandre Dumas</a> and Victor Hugo ... The volume I had was <i>The Man Who Laughs</i> — his face had been permanently operated on so that he will always have this perpetual grin. And it looked absolutely weird. I cut the picture out of the book and gave it to Bob, who drew the profile and gave it a more sinister aspect. Then he worked on the face; made him look a little clown-like, which accounted for his white face, red lips, green hair. And that was the Joker!<sup id="cite_ref-Batmania_14_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batmania_14-14">[14]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Although Kane adamantly refused to share credit for many of his characters (and refuted Robinson's claim until Kane's death), many comic historians credit Robinson with the Joker's creation and Finger with the character's development.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Creation1_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Creation4_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation4-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Creation2_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation2-9">[9]</a></sup> By 2011, Finger, Kane, and Robinson had died, leaving the story unresolved.<sup id="cite_ref-Creation5_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation5-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Creation2_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Creation2-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge6_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge6-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_Age">Golden Age</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comic_Book_-_The_Joker_(1940).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Comic book panel of the grinning Joker" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7b/Comic_Book_-_The_Joker_%281940%29.jpg/170px-Comic_Book_-_The_Joker_%281940%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7b/Comic_Book_-_The_Joker_%281940%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="316" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comic_Book_-_The_Joker_(1940).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From the Joker's debut in <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(comic_book)" title="Batman (comic book)">Batman</a></i> #1 (April 25, 1940)</div></div></div>
<p>The Joker debuted in <i>Batman</i> #1 (April 1940) as the eponymous character's first villain, about a year after Batman's debut in <i><a href="/wiki/Detective_Comics" title="Detective Comics">Detective Comics</a></i> #27 (May 1939). The Joker initially appeared as a remorseless serial killer and jewel thief, modeled after a joker playing card with a mirthless grin, who killed his victims with "Joker venom," a toxin that left their faces smiling grotesquely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201121_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201121-16">[16]</a></sup> The character was intended to be killed in his second appearance in <i>Batman</i> #1, after being stabbed in the heart. Finger wanted the Joker to die because of his concern that recurring villains would make Batman appear inept, but was overruled by then-editor <a href="/wiki/Whitney_Ellsworth" title="Whitney Ellsworth">Whitney Ellsworth</a>; a hastily drawn panel, indicating that the Joker was still alive, was added to the comic.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge1-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge3_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge3-18">[18]</a></sup> The Joker went on to appear in nine of <i>Batman</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s first 12 issues.<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge2_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge2-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The character's regular appearances quickly defined him as the archenemy of the Dynamic Duo – Batman and <a href="/wiki/Robin_(character)" title="Robin (character)">Robin</a>; he killed dozens of people, and even derailed a train.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> By issue #13, Kane's work on the <a href="/wiki/Print_syndication" title="Print syndication">syndicated</a> <i>Batman</i> newspaper strip left him little time for the comic book; artist <a href="/wiki/Dick_Sprang" title="Dick Sprang">Dick Sprang</a> assumed his duties, and editor Jack Schiff collaborated with Finger on stories. Around the same time, DC Comics found it easier to market its stories to children without the more mature <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp</a> elements that had originated many superhero comics. During this period, the first changes in the Joker began to appear, portraying him more as a prankster than a threat; when he kidnaps Robin, Batman pays the ransom by check, meaning that the Joker cannot cash it without being arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201124,_27_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201124,_27-21">[21]</a></sup> Comic book writer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Waid" title="Mark Waid">Mark Waid</a> suggests that the 1942 story "The Joker Walks the Last Mile" was the beginning point for the character's transformation into a more goofy incarnation, a period that <a href="/wiki/Grant_Morrison" title="Grant Morrison">Grant Morrison</a> considered to have lasted the following 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201536_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201536-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1942 cover of <i>Detective Comics</i> #69, known as "Double Guns" (with the Joker emerging from a genie lamp, aiming two guns at Batman and Robin), is considered one of the greatest superhero comic covers of the Golden Age and is the only image of the character using traditional guns. Robinson said that other contemporary villains used guns, and the creative team wanted the Joker—as Batman's adversary—to be more resourceful.<sup id="cite_ref-DoubleGuns_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleGuns-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DoubleGunsDeux_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleGunsDeux-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silver_Age">Silver Age</span></h3>
<p>The Joker was one of the few popular villains continuing to appear regularly in Batman comics from the Golden Age into the Silver Age, as the series continued during the rise in popularity of mystery and romance comics. In 1951, Finger wrote an <a href="/wiki/Origin_story" title="Origin story">origin story</a> for the Joker in <i>Detective Comics</i> #168, which introduced the characteristic of him formerly being the criminal <a href="/wiki/Red_Hood" title="Red Hood">Red Hood</a>, and his disfigurement the result of a fall into a chemical vat.<sup id="cite_ref-SilverAge1_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SilverAge1-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1954, the <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code Authority</a> had been established in response to increasing public disapproval of comic book content. The backlash was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Wertham" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederic Wertham">Frederic Wertham</a>, who hypothesized that mass media (especially comic books) was responsible for the rise in <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_delinquency" title="Juvenile delinquency">juvenile delinquency</a>, violence and homosexuality, particularly in young males. Parents forbade their children from reading comic books, and there were several mass burnings.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup> The Comics Code banned gore, innuendo and excessive violence, stripping Batman of his menace and transforming the Joker into a goofy, thieving trickster without his original homicidal tendencies.<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge1_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge1-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011171_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011171-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The character appeared less frequently after 1964, when <a href="/wiki/Julius_Schwartz" title="Julius Schwartz">Julius Schwartz</a> (who disliked the Joker) became editor of the Batman comics.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge1_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge1-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge5_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge5-27">[27]</a></sup> The character risked becoming an obscure figure of the preceding era until this goofy prankster version of the character was adapted into the 1966 television series <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(TV_series)" title="Batman (TV series)">Batman</a></i>, in which he was played by <a href="/wiki/Cesar_Romero" title="Cesar Romero">Cesar Romero</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge1_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge1-17">[17]</a></sup> The show's popularity compelled Schwartz to keep the comics in a similar vein. As the show's popularity waned, however, so did that of the <i>Batman</i> comics.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge5_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge5-27">[27]</a></sup> After the TV series ended in 1969, the increase in public visibility had not stopped the comic's sales decline; editorial director Carmine Infantino resolved to turn things around, moving stories away from schoolboy-friendly adventures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011173_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011173-28">[28]</a></sup> The Silver Age introduced several of the Joker's defining character traits: lethal <a href="/wiki/Joy_buzzer" title="Joy buzzer">joy buzzers</a>, acid-squirting flowers, trick guns, and goofy, elaborate crimes.<sup id="cite_ref-Personality2_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Personality2-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JokerGadgets_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerGadgets-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_(1973).jpg" class="image"><img alt=""Batman" cover, with the Joker holding an ace of spades with Batman on it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_%281973%29.jpg/170px-Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_%281973%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_%281973%29.jpg/255px-Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_%281973%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/69/Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_%281973%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="258" data-file-height="386" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comic_Book_-_Batman_251_Cover_(1973).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of <i>Batman</i> #251 (September 1973) featuring "The Joker's Five-Way Revenge", which returned the Joker to his homicidal roots. Art by Neal Adams.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1973, after a four-year disappearance,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup> the Joker was revived (and revised) by writer <a href="/wiki/Dennis_O%27Neil" title="Dennis O'Neil">Dennis O'Neil</a> and artist <a href="/wiki/Neal_Adams" title="Neal Adams">Neal Adams</a>. Beginning with <i>Batman</i> #251's "The Joker's Five-Way Revenge", the character returns to his roots as an impulsive, homicidal maniac who matches wits with Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge6_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge6-32">[32]</a></sup> This story began a trend in which the Joker was used, sparingly, as a central character.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge10_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge10-33">[33]</a></sup> O'Neil said his idea was "simply to take it back to where it started. I went to the DC library and read some of the early stories. I tried to get a sense of what Kane and Finger were after."<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge8_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge8-34">[34]</a></sup> O'Neil's 1973 run introduced the idea of the Joker being legally insane, to explain why the character is sent to <a href="/wiki/Arkham_Asylum" title="Arkham Asylum">Arkham Asylum</a> (introduced by O'Neil in 1974 as Arkham Hospital) instead of to prison.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge20_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge20-35">[35]</a></sup> Adams modified the Joker's appearance, changing his more average figure by extending his jaw and making him taller and leaner.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge9_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge9-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>DC Comics was a hotbed of experimentation during the 1970s, and in 1975 the character became the first villain to feature as the title character in a comic book series, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Joker_(comic_book)" title="The Joker (comic book)">The Joker</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011176_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011176-37">[37]</a></sup> The series followed the character's interactions with other supervillains, and the first issue was written by O'Neil.<sup id="cite_ref-1975JokerComic_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1975JokerComic-38">[38]</a></sup> Stories balanced between emphasizing the Joker's criminality and making him a likable protagonist whom readers could support. Although he murdered thugs and civilians, he never fought Batman; this made <i>The Joker</i> a series in which the character's villainy prevailed over rival villains, instead of a struggle between good and evil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011177_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011177-39">[39]</a></sup> Because the Comics Code Authority mandated punishment for villains, each issue ended with the Joker being apprehended, limiting the scope of each story. The series never found an audience, and <i>The Joker</i> was canceled after nine issues (despite a "next issue" advertisement for an appearance by the <a href="/wiki/Justice_League" title="Justice League">Justice League</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-1975JokerComic_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1975JokerComic-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011177_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011177-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncan_Smith2013380_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncan_Smith2013380-40">[40]</a></sup> The complete series became difficult to obtain over time, often commanding high prices from collectors. In 2013, DC Comics reissued the series as a <a href="/wiki/Trade_paperback_(comics)" title="Trade paperback (comics)">trade paperback</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XVI_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XVI-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Jenette_Kahn" title="Jenette Kahn">Jenette Kahn</a> became DC editor in 1976, she redeveloped the company's struggling titles; during her tenure, the Joker would become one of DC's most popular characters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011177_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011177-39">[39]</a></sup> While O'Neil and Adams' work was critically acclaimed, writer <a href="/wiki/Steve_Englehart" title="Steve Englehart">Steve Englehart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penciller" title="Penciller">penciller</a> <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Rogers" title="Marshall Rogers">Marshall Rogers</a>'s eight-issue run in <i>Detective Comics</i> #471–476 (August 1977–April 1978) defined the Joker for decades to come<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup> with stories emphasizing the character's insanity. In "The Laughing Fish", the Joker disfigures fish with a rictus grin resembling his own (expecting <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a> protection), and is unable to understand that copyrighting a <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resource</a> is legally impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge6_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge6-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge20_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge20-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bio101_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bio101-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge7_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge7-43">[43]</a></sup> Englehart's and Rogers' work on the series influenced the 1989 film <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(1989_film)" title="Batman (1989 film)">Batman</a></i>, and was adapted for 1992's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Animated_Series" title="Batman: The Animated Series">Batman: The Animated Series</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge20_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge20-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge4_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge4-44">[44]</a></sup> Rogers expanded on Adams' character design, drawing the Joker with a fedora and trench coat.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge9_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge9-36">[36]</a></sup> Englehart outlined how he understood the character by saying that the Joker "was this very crazy, scary character. I really wanted to get back to the idea of Batman fighting insane murderers at 3 a.m. under the full moon, as the clouds scuttled by."<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge1_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge1-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Age"><span class="anchor" id="Modern_age"></span>Modern Age</span></h3>
<p>Years after the end of the 1966 television series, sales of <i>Batman</i> continued to fall and the title was nearly cancelled. Although the 1970s restored the Joker as an insane, lethal foe of Batman, it was during the 1980s that the <i>Batman</i> series started to turn around and the Joker came into his own as part of the "Dark Age" of comics: mature tales of death and destruction. The shift was derided for moving away from tamer superheroes (and villains), but comic audiences were no longer primarily children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011182_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011182-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup> Several months after <i><a href="/wiki/Crisis_on_Infinite_Earths" title="Crisis on Infinite Earths">Crisis on Infinite Earths</a></i> launched the era by killing off Silver Age icons such as the <a href="/wiki/Flash_(DC_Comics_character)" title="Flash (DC Comics character)">Flash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supergirl" title="Supergirl">Supergirl</a> and undoing decades of continuity,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011183_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011183-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Miller_(comics)" title="Frank Miller (comics)">Frank Miller</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Returns" title="The Dark Knight Returns">The Dark Knight Returns</a></i> (1986) re-imagined Batman as an older, retired hero<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge2_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge2-47">[47]</a></sup> and the Joker as a lipstick-wearing celebrity<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge9_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge9-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011183_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011183-46">[46]</a></sup> who cannot function without his foe.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge4_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge4-48">[48]</a></sup> The late 1980s saw the Joker exert a significant impact on Batman and his supporting cast. In the 1988–89 story arc "<a href="/wiki/Batman:_A_Death_in_the_Family" title="Batman: A Death in the Family">A Death in the Family</a>", the Joker murders Batman's sidekick (the second Robin, <a href="/wiki/Jason_Todd" title="Jason Todd">Jason Todd</a>). Todd was unpopular with fans; rather than modify his character, DC opted to let them vote for his fate and a 72-vote plurality had the Joker beat Todd to death with a crowbar. This story altered the Batman universe: instead of killing anonymous bystanders, the Joker murdered a core character in the <i>Batman</i> fiction; this had a lasting effect on future stories.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge5_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge5-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011108_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011108-50">[50]</a></sup> Written at the height of tensions between the United States and Iran, the story's conclusion had Iranian leader <a href="/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini" title="Ruhollah Khomeini">Ayatollah Khomeini</a> appoint the Joker his country's ambassador to the United Nations (allowing him to temporarily escape justice).<sup id="cite_ref-InfoDoG1_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InfoDoG1-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Bolland" title="Brian Bolland">Brian Bolland</a>'s 1988 graphic novel <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Killing_Joke" title="Batman: The Killing Joke">The Killing Joke</a></i> expands on the Joker's origins, describing the character as a failed comedian who adopts the identity of the Red Hood to support his pregnant wife.<sup id="cite_ref-SilverAge1_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SilverAge1-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Unlike <i>The Dark Knight Returns</i>, <i>The Killing Joke</i> takes place in mainstream continuity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011188_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011188-53">[53]</a></sup> The novel is described by critics as one of the greatest Joker stories ever written, influencing later comic stories (including the forced retirement of then-<a href="/wiki/Barbara_Gordon" title="Barbara Gordon">Batgirl</a> Barbara Gordon after she is paralyzed by the Joker) and films such as 1989's <i>Batman</i> and 2008's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">The Dark Knight</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge10_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge10-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge11_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge11-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModernAgeCBR_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAgeCBR-56">[56]</a></sup> Grant Morrison's 1989 <i><a href="/wiki/Arkham_Asylum:_A_Serious_House_on_Serious_Earth" title="Arkham Asylum: A Serious House on Serious Earth">Arkham Asylum: A Serious House on Serious Earth</a></i> explores the psychoses of Batman, the Joker and other rogues in the eponymous facility.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge12_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge12-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge13_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge13-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1992 animated series introduced the Joker's female sidekick: <a href="/wiki/Harley_Quinn" title="Harley Quinn">Harley Quinn</a>, a psychiatrist who falls for—and ends up in an abusive relationship with—the Joker, becoming his <a href="/wiki/Supervillain" title="Supervillain">supervillain</a> accomplice. The character was popular, and was adapted into the comics as the Joker's romantic interest in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge15_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge15-59">[59]</a></sup> In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Grant_(writer)" title="Alan Grant (writer)">Alan Grant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norm_Breyfogle" title="Norm Breyfogle">Norm Breyfogle</a>'s comic book <i><a href="/wiki/Anarky_(comic_book)" title="Anarky (comic book)">Anarky</a></i> concluded with the revelation that <a href="/wiki/Anarky" title="Anarky">the titular character</a> was the Joker's son. Breyfogle conceived the idea as a means to expand on Anarky's characterization, but O'Neil (by then the editor for the <i>Batman</i> series of books) was opposed to it, and only allowed it to be written under protest, and with a promise that the revelation would eventually be revealed incorrect. However, the <i>Anarky</i> series was cancelled before the rebuttal could be published.<sup id="cite_ref-Anarky_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anarky-60">[60]</a></sup> The Joker's first major storyline in <a href="/wiki/The_New_52" title="The New 52">The New 52</a>, DC Comics' 2011 reboot of story continuity, was 2012's "<a href="/wiki/Death_of_the_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Death of the Family">Death of the Family</a>" by writer <a href="/wiki/Scott_Snyder" title="Scott Snyder">Scott Snyder</a> and artist <a href="/wiki/Greg_Capullo" title="Greg Capullo">Greg Capullo</a>. The story arc explores the <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic relationship</a> between the Joker and Batman, and sees the villain shatter the trust between Batman and his adopted family.<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge2_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge2-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge14_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge14-61">[61]</a></sup> Capullo's Joker design replaced his traditional outfit with a utilitarian, messy, and disheveled appearance to convey that the character was on a mission; his face (surgically removed in 2011's <i>Detective Comics</i> (vol. 2) #1) was reattached with belts, wires, and hooks, and he was outfitted with mechanics overalls.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAgeDOTF_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAgeDOTF-62">[62]</a></sup> The Joker's face was restored in Snyder's and Capullo's "<a href="/wiki/Batman:_Endgame" title="Batman: Endgame">Endgame</a>" (2014), the concluding chapter to "Death of the Family".<sup id="cite_ref-NewsaramaEndgame_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewsaramaEndgame-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The conclusion of the 2020 "<a href="/wiki/Joker_War" title="Joker War">Joker War</a>" storyline by writer <a href="/wiki/James_Tynion_IV" title="James Tynion IV">James Tynion IV</a> and artist Jorge Jiménez sees the Joker leave Gotham after Batman chooses to let him die.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> This leads into a second ongoing <i>Joker</i> series, set to begin in March 2021 with Tynion writing and Guillem March providing art.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Character_biography"><span class="anchor" id="Fictional_character_biography"></span>Character biography</span></h2>
<p>The Joker has undergone many revisions since his 1940 debut. The most common interpretation of the character is that he is disguised as the criminal Red Hood and pursued by Batman. The Joker falls into a vat of chemicals that bleaches his skin, colors his hair green and his lips red, and drives him insane. The reasons why the Joker was disguised as the Red Hood, and his identity before his transformation have changed over time.<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge1_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge1-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The character was introduced in <i>Batman</i> #1 (1940), in which he announces that he will kill three of Gotham's prominent citizens (including Henry Claridge). Although the police protect Claridge, the Joker had poisoned him before making his announcement and Claridge dies with a ghastly grin on his face; Batman eventually defeats him, sending him to prison.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek2_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek2-67">[67]</a></sup> The Joker commits whimsical, brutal crimes for reasons that, in Batman's words, "make sense to him alone".<sup id="cite_ref-Bio101_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bio101-42">[42]</a></sup> <i>Detective Comics</i> #168 (1951) introduced the Joker's first origin story as Red Hood: a criminal who, during his final heist, vanishes after leaping into a vat of chemicals to escape Batman. His resulting disfigurement led him to adopt the name "Joker", from the playing card figure he came to resemble.<sup id="cite_ref-SilverAge1_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SilverAge1-25">[25]</a></sup> The Joker's Silver Age transformation into a figure of fun was established in 1952's "<a href="/wiki/Joker%27s_Millions" title="Joker's Millions">The Joker's Millions</a>". In this story, the Joker is obsessed with maintaining his illusion of wealth and celebrity as a criminal <a href="/wiki/Folk_hero" title="Folk hero">folk hero</a>, afraid to let Gotham's citizens know that he is penniless and was tricked out of his fortune.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201127_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201127-68">[68]</a></sup> The 1970s redefined the character as a homicidal sociopath. "The Joker's Five-Way Revenge" has the Joker taking violent revenge on the former gang members who betrayed him;<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge10_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge10-33">[33]</a></sup> in "The Laughing Fish," the character chemically adds his face to Gotham's fish (hoping to profit from a copyright), killing bureaucrats who stand in his way.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge6_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge6-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alan_Moore_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="An older caucasian male with long, thick hair and matching beard, sits facing the camera." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Alan_Moore_%282%29.jpg/170px-Alan_Moore_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Alan_Moore_%282%29.jpg/255px-Alan_Moore_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Alan_Moore_%282%29.jpg/340px-Alan_Moore_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2135" data-file-height="2735" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alan_Moore_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Killing Joke</i> author <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moore" title="Alan Moore">Alan Moore</a> in 2008. The novel has been described as the greatest Joker story ever told.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge10_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge10-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge11_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge11-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ModernAgeCBR_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAgeCBR-56">[56]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><i>Batman: The Killing Joke</i> (1988) built on the Joker's 1951 origin story, portraying him as a failed comedian pressured into committing crime as the Red Hood to support his pregnant wife. Batman's interference causes him to leap into a chemical vat, which disfigures him. This, combined with the trauma of his wife's earlier accidental death, causes him to go insane and become the Joker.<sup id="cite_ref-SilverAge1_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SilverAge1-25">[25]</a></sup> However, the Joker says that this story may not be true, as he prefers his past to be "multiple choice".<sup id="cite_ref-TKJ_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TKJ-69">[69]</a></sup> In this graphic novel, the Joker shoots and paralyzes Barbara Gordon and tortures her father, <a href="/wiki/James_Gordon_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Gordon (comics)">Commissioner James Gordon</a>, to prove that it only takes one bad day to drive a normal man insane.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011188_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011188-53">[53]</a></sup> After Batman rescues Gordon and subdues the Joker, he offers to rehabilitate his old foe and end their rivalry. Although the Joker refuses, he shows his appreciation by sharing a joke with Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-PersonalityTKJ_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PersonalityTKJ-70">[70]</a></sup> Following the character's maiming of Barbara, she became a more important character in the DC Universe: the Oracle, a data gatherer and superhero informant, who has her revenge in <i><a href="/wiki/Birds_of_Prey_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of Prey (comics)">Birds of Prey</a></i> by shattering the Joker's teeth and destroying his smile.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011188_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011188-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1988 story "A Death in the Family", the Joker beats Jason Todd with a crowbar and leaves him to die in an explosion. Todd's death haunts Batman, and for the first time he considers killing the Joker.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge5_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge5-49">[49]</a></sup> The Joker temporarily escapes justice when Ayatollah <a href="/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini" title="Ruhollah Khomeini">Ruhollah Khomeini</a> appoints him the Iranian ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, giving him diplomatic immunity; however, when he tries to poison the U.N. membership, he is defeated by Batman and <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1999 "<a href="/wiki/No_Man%27s_Land_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="No Man's Land (comics)">No Man's Land</a>" storyline, the Joker murders Commissioner Gordon's second wife, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Essen_Gordon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarah Essen Gordon">Sarah</a>, as she shields a group of infants.<sup id="cite_ref-ComicNoManLand_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicNoManLand-71">[71]</a></sup> He taunts Gordon, who shoots him in the kneecap. The Joker, lamenting that he may never walk again, collapses with laughter when he realizes that the commissioner has avenged Barbara's paralysis.<sup id="cite_ref-DetectiveComic741_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DetectiveComic741-72">[72]</a></sup> This story also introduced the Joker's girlfriend, Harley Quinn.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge15_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge15-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2000s began with the <a href="/wiki/Fictional_crossover" class="mw-redirect" title="Fictional crossover">crossover</a> story "Emperor Joker", in which the Joker steals <a href="/wiki/Mister_Mxyzptlk" title="Mister Mxyzptlk">Mister Mxyzptlk</a>'s reality-altering power and remakes the universe in his image (torturing and killing Batman daily, before resurrecting him). When the supervillain then tries to destroy the universe, his reluctance to eliminate Batman makes him lose control, and Superman defeats him.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek3_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek3-73">[73]</a></sup> Broken by his experience, Batman's experiences of death are transferred to Superman by the <a href="/wiki/Spectre_(DC_Comics_character)#Hal_Jordan,_Spirit_of_Redemption" title="Spectre (DC Comics character)">Spectre</a> so he can heal mentally.<sup id="cite_ref-ComicEmperorJoker_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicEmperorJoker-74">[74]</a></sup> <span class="anchor" id="Last_Laugh"></span> In <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Laugh_(comics)" title="Last Laugh (comics)">Joker: Last Laugh</a></i> (2001), the doctors at <a href="/wiki/Arkham_Asylum" title="Arkham Asylum">Arkham Asylum</a> convince the character that he is dying in an attempt to rehabilitate him. Instead, the Joker (flanked by an army of "Jokerized" supervillains) launches a final crime spree. Believing that Robin (<a href="/wiki/Tim_Drake" title="Tim Drake">Tim Drake</a>) has been killed in the chaos, <a href="/wiki/Dick_Grayson" title="Dick Grayson">Dick Grayson</a> beats the Joker to death (although Batman revives his foe to keep Grayson from being a murderer), and the villain succeeds in making a member of the Bat-family break their rule against killing.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek2_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In "<a href="/wiki/Batman:_Under_the_Hood" title="Batman: Under the Hood">Under the Hood</a>" (2005), a resurrected Todd tries to force Batman to avenge his death by killing the Joker. Batman refuses, arguing that if he allows himself to kill the Joker, he will not be able to stop killing other criminals.<sup id="cite_ref-ComicUndertheHood_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicUndertheHood-75">[75]</a></sup> The Joker kills <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Luthor,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Luthor, Jr.">Alexander Luthor, Jr.</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Infinite_Crisis" title="Infinite Crisis">Infinite Crisis</a></i> (2005) for excluding him from the <a href="/wiki/Secret_Society_of_Super_Villains" title="Secret Society of Super Villains">Secret Society of Super Villains</a>, which considers him too unpredictable for membership.<sup id="cite_ref-ComicInfiniteCrisis7_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicInfiniteCrisis7-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DoGIC_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoGIC-77">[77]</a></sup> In Morrison's "<a href="/wiki/Batman_and_Son" title="Batman and Son">Batman and Son</a>" (2006), a deranged police officer who impersonates Batman shoots the Joker in the face, scarring and disabling him. The supervillain returns in "The Clown at Midnight" (2007) as a cruel, enigmatic force who awakens and tries to kill Harley Quinn to prove to Batman that he has become more than human.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011195_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011195-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup> In the 2008 story arc "<a href="/wiki/Batman_R.I.P." title="Batman R.I.P.">Batman R.I.P.</a>" the Joker is recruited by the Black Glove to destroy Batman, but betrays the group, killing its members one by one.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek2_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek2-67">[67]</a></sup> After Batman's apparent death in <i><a href="/wiki/Final_Crisis" title="Final Crisis">Final Crisis</a></i> (2008), Grayson investigates a series of murders (which leads him to a disguised Joker).<sup id="cite_ref-ComicBatmanRobin12_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicBatmanRobin12-79">[79]</a></sup> The Joker is arrested, and then-Robin <a href="/wiki/Damian_Wayne" title="Damian Wayne">Damian Wayne</a> beats him with a crowbar, paralleling Todd's murder. When the Joker escapes, he attacks the Black Glove, burying its leader <a href="/wiki/Simon_Hurt" title="Simon Hurt">Simon Hurt</a> alive after the supervillain considers him a failure as an opponent; the Joker is then defeated by the recently returned Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-InfoCA1_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InfoCA1-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-InfoCA2_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InfoCA2-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ComicBatmanRobin16_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicBatmanRobin16-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In DC's <a href="/wiki/The_New_52" title="The New 52">The New 52</a>, a 2011 relaunch of its titles following <i><a href="/wiki/Flashpoint_(comics)" title="Flashpoint (comics)">Flashpoint</a></i>, the Joker has his own face cut off.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek-83">[83]</a></sup> He disappears for a year, returning to launch an attack on Batman's extended family in "Death of the Family" so he and Batman can be the best hero and villain they can be.<sup id="cite_ref-DotF1_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DotF1-84">[84]</a></sup> At the end of the storyline, the Joker falls off a cliff into a dark abyss.<sup id="cite_ref-DotF1_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DotF1-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DOTFGuardian_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOTFGuardian-85">[85]</a></sup> The Joker returns in the 2014 storyline "Endgame" in which he brainwashes the Justice League into attacking Batman, believing he has betrayed their relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNEndgame_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNEndgame-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGNEndgame2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNEndgame2-87">[87]</a></sup> The story implies that the Joker is immortal—having existed for centuries in Gotham as a cause of tragedy after exposure to a substance the Joker terms 'dionesium'—and is able to regenerate from mortal injuries. "Endgame" restores the Joker's face, and also reveals that he knows Batman's secret identity.<sup id="cite_ref-NewsaramaEndgame_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewsaramaEndgame-63">[63]</a></sup> The story ends with the apparent deaths of Batman and the Joker at each other's hands, though it is revealed that they were both resurrected in a life-restoring <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_Pit" class="mw-redirect" title="Lazarus Pit">Lazarus Pit</a>, without their memories.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PostEndGameRama_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PostEndGameRama-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the "<a href="/wiki/Darkseid_War" title="Darkseid War">Darkseid War</a>" (2015–2016) storyline, Batman uses <a href="/wiki/Metron_(comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metron (comics)">Metron</a>'s Mobius Chair to find out the Joker's real name; the chair's answer leaves Batman in disbelief. In the <i><a href="/wiki/DC_Rebirth" title="DC Rebirth">DC Universe: Rebirth</a></i> (2016) one-shot, Batman informs <a href="/wiki/Hal_Jordan" title="Hal Jordan">Hal Jordan</a> that the chair told him there were three individual Jokers, not just one.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> This revelation was the basis for the miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Three_Jokers" title="Batman: Three Jokers">Batman: Three Jokers</a></i> (2020), written by <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Johns" title="Geoff Johns">Geoff Johns</a> with art by <a href="/wiki/Jason_Fabok" title="Jason Fabok">Jason Fabok</a>. <i>Three Jokers</i> reveals that the three Jokers, who work in tandem, include the "Criminal", a criminal mastermind based on the Golden Age Joker; the "Clownish", a goofy prankster based on the Silver Age Joker; and the "Comedian", a psychotic killer based on the Modern Age Joker.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Todd executes the Clownish (who was the Joker who killed Todd in "A Death in the Family") and the Criminal is murdered by the Comedian, who was the original Joker. The miniseries ends with the reveal that Batman knows the Joker's true identity.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins"><span class="anchor" id="Various_origins"></span>Origins</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:27%; ; padding:10px; background-color: #EFEFEF;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1012376600">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"They've given many origins of the Joker, how he came to be. That doesn't seem to matter—just how he is now. I never intended to give a reason for his appearance. We discussed that and Bill [Finger] and I never wanted to change it at that time. I thought—and he agreed—that it takes away some of the essential mystery."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">– Jerry Robinson, the Joker's creator<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2012180_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2012180-92">[92]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Although a number of <a href="/wiki/Backstory" title="Backstory">backstories</a> have been given, a definitive one has never been established for the Joker. An <a href="/wiki/Unreliable_narrator" title="Unreliable narrator">unreliable narrator</a>, the character is uncertain of who he was before and how he became the Joker: "Sometimes I remember it one way, sometimes another ...if I'm going to have a past, I prefer it to be multiple choice!"<sup id="cite_ref-Origin3_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin3-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TKJ_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TKJ-69">[69]</a></sup> A story about the Joker's origin appeared in <i>Detective Comics</i> #168 (February 1951), nearly a decade after the character's debut. Here, the character is a laboratory worker who becomes the Red Hood (a masked criminal) to steal 1 million dollars and retire. He falls into a vat of chemical waste when his heist is thwarted by Batman, emerging with bleached white skin, red lips, green hair and a permanent grin.<sup id="cite_ref-Origin1_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin1-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Origin2_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>This story was the basis for the most often-cited origin tale, Moore's <a href="/wiki/One-shot_(comics)" title="One-shot (comics)">one-shot</a> <i>The Killing Joke</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge11_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge11-55">[55]</a></sup> The Joker quits his job as a lab assistant, becoming a stand-up comedian to support his pregnant wife. Unsuccessful, he agrees to help mobsters with a robbery and becomes the Red Hood. The heist goes awry; the comedian leaps into a chemical vat to escape Batman, surfacing disfigured. This, combined with the earlier accidental death of his wife and unborn child, drives the comedian insane and he becomes the Joker.<sup id="cite_ref-SilverAge1_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SilverAge1-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR2_31-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR2-31">[31]</a></sup> This version has been cited in many stories, including <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Man_Who_Laughs" title="Batman: The Man Who Laughs">Batman: The Man Who Laughs</a></i> (in which Batman deduces that the Red Hood survived his fall and became the Joker), <i>Batman</i> #450 (in which the Joker dons the Red Hood to aid his recovery after the events in "A Death in the Family", but finds the experience too traumatic), "Death of the Family",<sup id="cite_ref-Origin2_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-94">[94]</a></sup> and <i>Batman: Three Jokers</i> (which asserts that it is the <a href="/wiki/Canon_(fiction)" title="Canon (fiction)">canon</a> origin story).<sup id="cite_ref-IGNThreeJokersEnding_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNThreeJokersEnding-95">[95]</a></sup> Other stories have expanded on this origin; "<a href="/wiki/Batman:_Gotham_Knights" title="Batman: Gotham Knights">Pushback</a>" suggests that the Joker's wife was murdered by a corrupt policeman working for the mobsters,<sup id="cite_ref-OriginPushback_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OriginPushback-96">[96]</a></sup> and "Payback" gives the Joker's first name as "Jack".<sup id="cite_ref-Origin2_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-94">[94]</a></sup> The ending of <i>Batman: Three Jokers</i> establishes that the Joker's wife did not actually die—rather, she fled to Alaska with the help of Gotham police and Batman because she feared her husband would be an abusive father; the police then told the Joker a story about her dying to protect her. The miniseries also reveals that Batman knows the Joker's identity, and has kept it secret out of knowledge it would put his wife and son in danger.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNThreeJokersEnding_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNThreeJokersEnding-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the Joker's unreliable memory has allowed writers to develop other origins for the character.<sup id="cite_ref-Origin2_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-94">[94]</a></sup> "Case Study", a <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dini" title="Paul Dini">Paul Dini</a>-<a href="/wiki/Alex_Ross" title="Alex Ross">Alex Ross</a> story, describes the Joker as a sadistic gangster who creates the Red Hood identity to continue the thrill of small-time criminality. He has his fateful first meeting with Batman, which results in his disfigurement. It is suggested that the Joker is sane, and feigns insanity to avoid the death penalty. In <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_Confidential" title="Batman Confidential">Batman Confidential</a></i> #7–12, the character, Jack, is a talented criminal who is bored with his work. He encounters (and becomes obsessed with) Batman during a heist, embarking on a crime spree to attract his attention. After Jack injures Batman's girlfriend, Batman scars Jack's face with a <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_smile" title="Glasgow smile">permanent grin</a> and betrays him to a group of mobsters, who torture him in a chemical plant. Jack escapes, but falls into an empty vat as gunfire punctures chemical tanks above him. The flood of chemicals (used in <a href="/wiki/Anti-psychotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-psychotic">anti-psychotic</a> medication) alters his appearance and completes his transformation.<sup id="cite_ref-ComicBatmanConfidential_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicBatmanConfidential-97">[97]</a></sup> The superhero <a href="/wiki/Atom_(Ray_Palmer)" title="Atom (Ray Palmer)">Atom</a> sees the Joker's memory of burning his parents alive (after they find him killing animals) in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brave_and_the_Bold" title="The Brave and the Bold">The Brave and the Bold</a></i> #31,<sup id="cite_ref-BraveandBold31Review_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BraveandBold31Review-98">[98]</a></sup> and Snyder's "<a href="/wiki/Batman:_Zero_Year" title="Batman: Zero Year">Zero Year</a>" (2013) suggests that the pre-disfigured Joker was a criminal mastermind leading a gang of Red Hoods.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNEndgame_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNEndgame-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OriginZeroYear_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OriginZeroYear-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker has claimed a number of origins, including being the child of an abusive father who broke his nose, and the long-lived jester of an Egyptian pharaoh. As Batman says: "Like any other comedian, he uses whatever material will work."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011136_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011136-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_versions">Alternative versions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_versions_of_Joker" title="Alternative versions of Joker">Alternative versions of Joker</a></div>
<p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Parallel_universe_(fiction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Parallel universe (fiction)">alternate universes</a> in DC Comics publications allow writers to introduce variations on the Joker, in which the character's origins, behavior, and morality differ from the mainstream setting.<sup id="cite_ref-Multiverse_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Multiverse-101">[101]</a></sup> <i>The Dark Knight Returns</i> depicts the final battle between an aged Batman and Joker; others portray the aftermath of the Joker's death at the hands of a number of characters, including Superman.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek3_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek3-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OtherInjustice_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OtherInjustice-102">[102]</a></sup> Still others describe distant futures in which the Joker is a computer virus or a hero trying to defeat the era's tyrannical Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201138_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201138-103">[103]</a></sup> In some stories, the Joker is someone else entirely; <i><a href="/wiki/Flashpoint_(comics)" title="Flashpoint (comics)">Flashpoint</a></i> features Batman's mother <a href="/wiki/Martha_Wayne" title="Martha Wayne">Martha Wayne</a> as the Joker in response to her son's murder,<sup id="cite_ref-OtherFlashpoint_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OtherFlashpoint-104">[104]</a></sup> and in <i><a href="/wiki/Superman:_Speeding_Bullets" title="Superman: Speeding Bullets">Superman: Speeding Bullets</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Lex_Luthor" title="Lex Luthor">Lex Luthor</a> becomes the Joker in a world where Superman is Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-OtherSpeeding_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OtherSpeeding-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characterization">Characterization</span></h2>
<p>Renowned as Batman's greatest enemy,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactRama2013_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactRama2013-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GRadarGreatest_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRadarGreatest-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGNRogues_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNRogues-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DOGGreatest_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOGGreatest-109">[109]</a></sup> the Joker is known by a number of nicknames, including the Clown Prince of Crime, the Harlequin of Hate, the Ace of Knaves, and the Jester of Genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNRogues_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNRogues-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nicknames_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicknames-110">[110]</a></sup> During the evolution of the <a href="/wiki/DC_Universe" title="DC Universe">DC Universe</a>, interpretations and versions of the Joker have taken two main forms. The original, dominant image is that of an extreme <a href="/wiki/Psychopathy" title="Psychopathy">psychopath</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2012130_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2012130-111">[111]</a></sup> with genius-level intelligence and a warped, sadistic sense of humor.<sup id="cite_ref-CrackingUp_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrackingUp-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ComicsComix_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicsComix-113">[113]</a></sup> The other version, popular in comic books from the late 1940s to the 1960s and in the 1960s television series, is an eccentric, harmless prankster and thief.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201169_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201169-114">[114]</a></sup> Like other long-lived characters, the Joker's character and cultural interpretations have changed with time; however, unlike other characters who may need to reconcile or ignore previous versions to make sense, more than any other comic book character, the Joker thrives on his mutable and irreconcilable identities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201533_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201533-115">[115]</a></sup> The Joker is typically seen in a purple suit with a long-tailed, padded-shoulder jacket, a string tie, gloves, striped pants and <a href="/wiki/Spats_(footwear)" title="Spats (footwear)">spats</a> on pointed-toe shoes (sometimes with a wide-brimmed hat). This appearance is such a fundamental aspect of the character that when the 2004 animated series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Batman" title="The Batman">The Batman</a></i> placed the Joker in a straitjacket, it quickly redesigned him in his familiar suit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201169_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201169-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker is obsessed with Batman, the pair representing a yin-yang of opposing dark and light force; although it is the Joker who represents humor and color and Batman who dwells in the dark.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201137_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201137-116">[116]</a></sup> No crime – including murder, theft, and terrorism – is beyond the Joker, and his exploits are theatrical performances that are funny to him alone. Spectacle is more important than success for the Joker, and if it is not spectacular it is boring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XIX_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XIX-117">[117]</a></sup> Although the Joker claims indifference to everything, he secretly craves Batman's attention and validation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011105_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011105-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge6_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge6-32">[32]</a></sup> The character was described as having killed over 2,000 people in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Joker:_Devil%27s_Advocate" title="The Joker: Devil's Advocate">The Joker: Devil's Advocate</a></i> (1996). Despite this body count, he is always found <a href="/wiki/Insanity_defense" title="Insanity defense">not guilty by reason of insanity</a> and sent to Arkham Asylum, avoiding the death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerDevilsAdvocate_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerDevilsAdvocate-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2012162–163_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2012162–163-120">[120]</a></sup> Many of the Joker's acts attempt to force Batman to kill; if the most orderly and self-controlled of humans can murder, anyone is capable of becoming a monster like the Joker. The villain displays no instinct for self-preservation, and is willing to die to prove his point.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactMoral3_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactMoral3-121">[121]</a></sup> The Joker is the "personification of the irrational," and represents "everything Batman [opposes]."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ComicCon2008jerryrobinson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Smiling, bearded white-haired man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/ComicCon2008jerryrobinson.jpg/170px-ComicCon2008jerryrobinson.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/ComicCon2008jerryrobinson.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ComicCon2008jerryrobinson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Joker co-creator <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Robinson" title="Jerry Robinson">Jerry Robinson</a> in 2008; he conceived the Joker as an exotic, enduring archvillain who could repeatedly challenge Batman.</div></div></div>
<p>The Joker's main characteristic is his apparent insanity, although he is not described as having any particular psychological disorder. Like a psychopath, he lacks empathy, a conscience, and concern over right and wrong. In <i>Arkham Asylum: A Serious House on Serious Earth</i>, the Joker is described as capable of processing outside sensory information only by adapting to it. This enables him to create a new personality every day (depending on what would benefit him) and explains why, at different times, he is a mischievous clown or a psychopathic killer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2012180–181_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2012180–181-123">[123]</a></sup> In "The Clown at Midnight" (<i>Batman</i> #663 (April 2007)), the Joker enters a meditative state where he evaluates his previous selves to consciously create a new personality, effectively modifying himself for his needs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015217_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015217-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The Killing Joke</i> (in which the Joker is the <a href="/wiki/Unreliable_narrator" title="Unreliable narrator">unreliable narrator</a>) explains the roots of his insanity as "one bad day": losing his wife and unborn child and being disfigured by chemicals, paralleling Batman's origin in the loss of his parents. He tries (and fails) to prove that anyone can become like him after one bad day by torturing Commissioner Gordon, physically and psychologically.<sup id="cite_ref-Personality2_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Personality2-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011188_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011188-53">[53]</a></sup> Batman offers to rehabilitate his foe; the Joker apologetically declines, believing it too late for him to be saved.<sup id="cite_ref-PersonalityTKJ_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PersonalityTKJ-70">[70]</a></sup> Other interpretations show that the Joker is fully aware of how his actions affect others and that his insanity as merely an act.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201137_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201137-116">[116]</a></sup> Comics scholar <a href="/wiki/Peter_Coogan" title="Peter Coogan">Peter Coogan</a> describes the Joker as trying to reshape reality to fit himself by imposing his face on his victims (and fish) in an attempt to make the world comprehensible by creating a twisted parody of himself. Englehart's "The Laughing Fish" demonstrates the character's illogical nature: trying to copyright fish that bear his face, and not understanding why threatening the copyright clerk cannot produce the desired result.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge20_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge20-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2012183–184_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2012183–184-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker is alternatively depicted as sexual and asexual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncan_Smith2013379_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncan_Smith2013379-126">[126]</a></sup> In <i>The Dark Knight Returns</i> and <i>Arkham Asylum: A Serious House on Serious Earth</i>, the Joker is seductive toward Batman; it is uncertain if their relationship has <a href="/wiki/Homoerotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Homoerotic">homoerotic</a> undertones or if the Joker is simply trying to manipulate his nemesis. Frank Miller interpreted the character as fixated on death and uninterested in sexual relationships, while Robinson believed that the Joker is capable of a romantic relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncan_Smith2013379_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncan_Smith2013379-126">[126]</a></sup> His relationship with Harley Quinn is abusively paradoxical; although the Joker keeps her at his side, he heedlessly harms her (for example, throwing her out a window without seeing if she survives). Harley loves him, but the Joker does not reciprocate her feelings, chiding her for distracting him from other plans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangley2012177–180_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangley2012177–180-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>Snyder's "Death of the Family" describes the Joker as in love with Batman, although not in a traditionally romantic way.
