The Joker believes that Batman has not killed him because he makes Batman better and he loves the villain for that.<sup id="cite_ref-ModernAge14_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ModernAge14-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CharacterDOTF_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CharacterDOTF-128">[128]</a></sup> <i>Batman</i> comic book writer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Tomasi" title="Peter Tomasi">Peter Tomasi</a> concurred, stating that the Joker's main goal is to make Batman the best that he can be.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XXI_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XXI-129">[129]</a></sup> The Joker and Batman represent opposites: the extroverted Joker wears colorful clothing and embraces chaos, while the introverted, monochromatic Batman represents order and discipline. The Joker is often depicted as defining his existence through his conflict with Batman. In 1994's "Going Sane", the villain tries to lead a normal life after Batman's (apparent) death, only to become his old self again when Batman reappears; in "Emperor Joker", an apparently omnipotent Joker cannot destroy Batman without undoing himself. Since the Joker is simply "the Joker", he believes that Batman is "Batman" (with or without the costume) and has no interest in what is behind Batman's mask, ignoring opportunities to learn Batman's secret identity.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek3_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek3-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201137–38_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201137–38-130">[130]</a></sup> Given the opportunity to kill Batman, the villain demurs; he believes that without their game, winning is pointless.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011105_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011105-118">[118]</a></sup> The character has no desire for typical criminal goals like money or power; his criminality is designed only to continue his game with Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker is portrayed as having no fear; when fellow supervillain <a href="/wiki/Scarecrow_(DC_Comics)" title="Scarecrow (DC Comics)">Scarecrow</a> doses him with fear toxin in <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Knightfall" title="Batman: Knightfall">Knightfall</a></i> (1993), the Joker merely laughs and says "Boo!"<sup id="cite_ref-DetectiveComic664_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DetectiveComic664-131">[131]</a></sup> The villain has been temporarily rendered sane by several means, including <a href="/wiki/Telepathy" title="Telepathy">telepathic</a> manipulation by the <a href="/wiki/Martian_Manhunter" title="Martian Manhunter">Martian Manhunter</a><sup id="cite_ref-PersonalityTKJ_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PersonalityTKJ-70">[70]</a></sup> and being resurrected in a Lazarus Pit (an experience typically inducing temporary insanity in the subject). At these moments, the Joker is depicted as expressing remorse for his acts;<sup id="cite_ref-ComicLotDarkKnight145_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicLotDarkKnight145-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ComicJLA15_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicJLA15-133">[133]</a></sup> however, during a medically induced period of partial sanity in <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Cacophony" title="Batman: Cacophony">Batman: Cacophony</a></i> he tells his opponent, "I don't hate you 'cause I'm crazy. I'm crazy 'cause I hate you," and confirms that he will only stop murdering when Batman is dead.<sup id="cite_ref-CraveCacophony_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CraveCacophony-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ComicCacophony_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ComicCacophony-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skills_and_equipment">Skills and equipment</span></h3>
<p>The Joker has no inherent superhuman abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactWhatCulture_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactWhatCulture-136">[136]</a></sup> He commits crimes with a variety of weaponized thematic props such as a deck of razor-tipped playing cards, rolling marbles, <a href="/wiki/Jack-in-the-box" title="Jack-in-the-box">jack-in-the-boxes</a> with unpleasant surprises and exploding cigars capable of leveling a building. The flower in his lapel sprays acid, and his hand often holds a lethal joy buzzer conducting a million volts of electricity, although both items were introduced in 1952 as harmless joke items.<sup id="cite_ref-JokerGadgets_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerGadgets-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201170_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201170-137">[137]</a></sup> However, his chemical genius provides his most-notable weapon: Joker venom, a liquid or gaseous toxin that sends its targets into fits of uncontrollable laughter; higher doses can lead to paralysis, coma or death, leaving its victim with a ghoulish, pained rictus grin. The Joker has used venom since his debut; only he knows the formula, and is shown to be gifted enough to manufacture the toxin from ordinary household chemicals. Another version of the venom (used in <i>Joker: Last Laugh</i>) makes its victims resemble the Joker, susceptible to his orders.<sup id="cite_ref-BronzeAge6_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BronzeAge6-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JokerBioGeek2_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerBioGeek2-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WeaponsVenom_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WeaponsVenom-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201169,_124_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201169,_124-139">[139]</a></sup> The villain is immune to venom and most poisons; in <i>Batman</i> #663 (April 2007), Morrison writes that being "an avid consumer of his own chemical experiments, the Joker's immunity to poison concoctions that might kill another man in an instant has been developed over years of dedicated abuse."<sup id="cite_ref-Batman663_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batman663-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201169_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201169-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The character's arsenal is inspired by his nemesis' weaponry, such as <a href="/wiki/Batarang" title="Batarang">batarangs</a>. In "The Joker's Utility Belt" (1952), he mimicked <a href="/wiki/Batman%27s_utility_belt" title="Batman's utility belt">Batman's utility belt</a> with non-lethal items, such as Mexican jumping beans and sneezing powder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201170_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201170-137">[137]</a></sup> In 1942's "The Joker Follows Suit", the villain built his versions of the <a href="/wiki/Batplane" title="Batplane">Batplane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Batmobile" title="Batmobile">Batmobile</a>, the Jokergyro and Jokermobile (the latter with a large Joker face on its hood), and created a Joker-signal with which criminals could summon him for their heists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201170,75_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201170,75-141">[141]</a></sup> The Jokermobile lasted for several decades, evolving with the Batmobile. His technical genius is not limited by practicality, allowing him to hijack Gotham's television airwaves to issue threats, transform buildings into death traps, launch a gas attack on the city and rain poisoned glass shards on its citizens from an airship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201175_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201175-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGNJokerStories_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNJokerStories-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker is portrayed as skilled in <a href="/wiki/Melee" title="Melee">melee</a> combat, from his initial appearances when he defeats Batman in a sword fight (nearly killing him), and others when he overwhelms Batman but declines to kill him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201124,_37–38,_105_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201124,_37–38,_105-144">[144]</a></sup> He is talented with firearms, although even his guns are theatrical; his long-barreled revolver often releases a flag reading "Bang", and a second trigger-pull launches the flag to skewer its target.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201170_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201170-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Debut_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debut-145">[145]</a></sup> Although formidable in combat, the Joker's chief asset is his mind.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201138_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201138-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships">Relationships</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Batman_family_enemies" title="List of Batman family enemies">List of Batman family enemies</a></div>
<p>The Joker's unpredictable, homicidal nature makes him one of the most feared supervillains in the DC Universe; the <a href="/wiki/Trickster_(comics)" title="Trickster (comics)">Trickster</a> says in the 1995 miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/Underworld_Unleashed" title="Underworld Unleashed">Underworld Unleashed</a></i>, "When super-villains want to scare each other, they tell Joker stories."<sup id="cite_ref-BatmanUnauthorized_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BatmanUnauthorized-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UnderworldUnleashed_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UnderworldUnleashed-147">[147]</a></sup> Gotham's villains also feel threatened by the character; depending on the circumstances, he is as likely to fight with his rivals for control of the city as he is to join them for an entertaining outcome.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201183_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201183-148">[148]</a></sup> The Joker interacts with other supervillains who oppose Batman, whether he is on the streets or in Arkham Asylum. He has collaborated with criminals like the <a href="/wiki/Penguin_(character)" title="Penguin (character)">Penguin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Riddler" title="Riddler">Riddler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Two-Face" title="Two-Face">Two-Face</a>, although these partnerships rarely end well due to the Joker's desire for unbridled chaos, and he uses his stature to lead others (such as <a href="/wiki/Killer_Croc" title="Killer Croc">Killer Croc</a> and the Scarecrow).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201183,_86_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201183,_86-149">[149]</a></sup> The Joker's greatest rival is the smartest man in the world, <a href="/wiki/Lex_Luthor" title="Lex Luthor">Lex Luthor</a>. Although they have a friendly partnership in 1950's <i>World's Finest Comics</i> #88, later unions emphasized their mutual hostility and clashing egos.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201186,93_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201186,93-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite his tendency to kill subordinates on a whim, the Joker has no difficulty attracting henchmen with a seemingly infinite cash supply and intimidation: they are too afraid of their employer to refuse his demands that they wear red clown noses or laugh at macabre jokes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201175_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201175-142">[142]</a></sup> Even with his unpredictability and lack of superhuman powers, the 2007 limited series <i>Salvation Run</i> sees hundreds of villains fall under his spell because they are more afraid of him than the alternative: Luthor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011124,_126_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011124,_126-151">[151]</a></sup> <i>Batman</i> #186 (1966) introduced the Joker's first sidekick: the one-shot character Gaggy Gagsworthy, who is short and dressed like a clown; the character was later resurrected as an enemy of his replacement, Harley Quinn.<sup id="cite_ref-SidekickGaggy_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SidekickGaggy-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SidekickGaggy2_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SidekickGaggy2-153">[153]</a></sup> Introduced in the 1992 animated series, Quinn is the Joker's former Arkham psychiatrist who develops an obsessive infatuation with him and dons a red-and-black <a href="/wiki/Harlequin" title="Harlequin">harlequin</a> costume to join him in the 1999 graphic novel <i>Batman: Harley Quinn</i>. Although Quinn loves the Joker, he is obsessed with Batman and uses her to achieve his goals at her expense. Despite violent abuse, she returns to him.<sup id="cite_ref-SidekickGaggy2_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SidekickGaggy2-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011143,_152_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011143,_152-154">[154]</a></sup> The Joker is sometimes shown to keep <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyenas</a> as pets; this trait was introduced in the 1977 animated series <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Adventures_of_Batman" title="The New Adventures of Batman">The New Adventures of Batman</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201175_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201175-142">[142]</a></sup> A 1976 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_Family" title="Batman Family">Batman Family</a></i> introduced <a href="/wiki/Duela_Dent" title="Duela Dent">Duela Dent</a> as the Joker's daughter, though her parentage claim was later proven to be false.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011177_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011177-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although his chief obsession is Batman, the character has occasionally ventured outside Gotham City to fight Batman's superhero allies. In "To Laugh and Die in Metropolis" (1987) the character kidnaps <a href="/wiki/Lois_Lane" title="Lois Lane">Lois Lane</a>, distracting Superman with a nuclear weapon. The story is notable for the Joker taking on a (relative) god and the ease with which Superman defeats him—it took only 17 pages. Asked why he came to Metropolis, the Joker replies simply: "Oh Superman, why not?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011121_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011121-155">[155]</a></sup> In 1995, the Joker fought his third major DC hero: <a href="/wiki/Wonder_Woman" title="Wonder Woman">Wonder Woman</a>, who drew on the Greek god of trickery to temper the Joker's humor and shatter his confidence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2011121–122,_124_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2011121–122,_124-156">[156]</a></sup> The character has joined supervillain groups like the Injustice Gang and the Injustice League to take on superhero groups like the Justice League.<sup id="cite_ref-Teamup1_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teamup1-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Teamup2_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teamup2-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_analysis">Literary analysis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joker_expo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large playing card bearing the Joker's face stands before a series of art works featuring the Joker" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Joker_expo.jpg/220px-Joker_expo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Joker_expo.jpg/330px-Joker_expo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Joker_expo.jpg/440px-Joker_expo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joker_expo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 2015 art exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Barcelona_International_Comics_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Barcelona International Comics Convention">Barcelona International Comics Convention</a> focused on the Joker, celebrating the character's 75th anniversary.<sup id="cite_ref-SalondeBarcelona_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalondeBarcelona-159">[159]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Since the Bronze Age of Comics, the Joker has been interpreted as an archetypal <a href="/wiki/Trickster" title="Trickster">trickster</a>, displaying talents for cunning intelligence, social engineering, pranks, theatricality, and <a href="/wiki/Idiom" title="Idiom">idiomatic</a> humor. Like the trickster, the Joker alternates between malicious violence and clever, harmless whimsy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015109,_195_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015109,_195-160">[160]</a></sup> He is amoral and not driven by ethical considerations, but by a shameless and insatiable nature, and although his actions are condemned as evil, he is necessary for cultural robustness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015111_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015111-161">[161]</a></sup> The trickster employs amoral and immoral acts to destabilize the status quo and reveal cultural, political, and ethical hypocrisies that society attempts to ignore.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015109_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015109-162">[162]</a></sup> However, the Joker differs in that his actions typically only benefit himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015112_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015112-163">[163]</a></sup> The Joker possesses abnormal body imagery, reflecting an inversion of order. The trickster is simultaneously subhuman and superhuman, a being that indicates a lack of unity in body and mind.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015195_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015195-164">[164]</a></sup> In <i>Arkham Asylum: A Serious House on Serious Earth</i>, the Joker serves as Batman's trickster guide through the hero's own psyche, testing him in various ways before ultimately offering to cede his rule of the Asylum to Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015205_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015205-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rather than the typical <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchist</a> interpretation, others have analysed the character as a <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> (opposite to Batman's capitalist), arguing that anarchism requires the rejection of all authority in favor of uncontrolled freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201595_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201595-166">[166]</a></sup> The Joker rejects most authority, but retains his own, using his actions to coerce and consolidate power in himself and convert the masses to his own way of thinking, while eliminating any that oppose him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201521_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201521-167">[167]</a></sup> In <i>The Killing Joke</i>, the Joker is an abused member of the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">underclass</a> who is driven insane by failings of the social system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201596_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201596-168">[168]</a></sup> The Joker rejects material needs, and his first appearance in <i>Batman</i> #1 sees him perpetrate crimes against Gotham's wealthiest men and the judge who had sent him to prison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201598_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201598-169">[169]</a></sup> Batman is wealthy, yet the Joker is able to triumph through his own innovations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201599_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201599-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ryan Litsey described the Joker as an example of a "<a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">Nietzschean Superman</a>," arguing that a fundamental aspect of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>'s <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbermensch" title="Übermensch">Superman</a>, the "will to power," is exemplified in all of the Joker's actions, providing a <a href="/wiki/Master%E2%80%93slave_morality" title="Master–slave morality">master morality</a> to Batman's slave morality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201522_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201522-171">[171]</a></sup> The character's indomitable "will to power" means he is never discouraged by being caught or defeated and he is not restrained by guilt or remorse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015185_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015185-172">[172]</a></sup> Joker represents the master, who creates rules and defines them, who judges others without needing approval, and for whom something is good because it benefits him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015185–6_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015185–6-173">[173]</a></sup> He creates his own morality and is bound only by his own rules without aspiring to something higher than himself, unlike Batman, the slave, who makes a distinction between good and evil, and is bound to rules outside of himself (such as his avoidance of killing) in his quest for justice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015187–8_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015187–8-174">[174]</a></sup> The Joker has no defined origin story that requires him to question how he came to be, as like the Superman he does not regret or assess the past and only moves forward.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015189_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015189-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker's controlling and abusive relationship with Harley Quinn has been analyzed as a means of the Joker reinforcing his own belief in his power in a world where he may be killed or neutralized by another villain or Batman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201583–84_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201583–84-176">[176]</a></sup> Joker mirrors his identity through Harley in her appearance, and even though he may ignore or act indifferent towards her, he continues to try to subject her to his control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201583–84_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201583–84-176">[176]</a></sup> When Harley successfully defeats Batman in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Batman_Adventures:_Mad_Love" title="The Batman Adventures: Mad Love">Mad Love</a></i> (1994), the Joker, emasculated by his own failure, severely injures her out of fear of what the other villains will think of him; however, while Harley recovers, the Joker sends her flowers, which she accepts, reasserting his control over her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201586–87_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201586–87-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Harley's co-creator, Paul Dini, describes their relationship as Harley being someone who makes the Joker feel better about himself, and who can do the work that he does not want to do himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201587_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201587-178">[178]</a></sup> In the 1999 one-shot comic <i>Batman: Harley Quinn</i>, the Joker decides to kill Harley, after admitting that he does care for her, that their relationship is romantic, and that these feelings prevent him from fulfilling his purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201585–86_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201585–86-179">[179]</a></sup> Removing the traditional male-female relationship, such as in the <i>Batman: Thrillkiller</i> storyline where the Joker (Bianca Steeplechase) is a female and involved in a <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbian</a> relationship with Harley, their relationship lacks any aspects of violence or subjugation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201590–91_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201590–91-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact_and_legacy">Cultural impact and legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Three smiling live-action villains next to electronic equipment" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG/170px-Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG/255px-Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG/340px-Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1110" data-file-height="1290" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Batman_villains_The_Penguin_The_Riddler_The_Joker_1967.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cesar_Romero" title="Cesar Romero">Cesar Romero</a> as the Joker in the 1966 movie <i>Batman</i> (based on the TV series of the same name), with <a href="/wiki/Burgess_Meredith" title="Burgess Meredith">Burgess Meredith</a> (left) as the Penguin and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Gorshin" title="Frank Gorshin">Frank Gorshin</a> as the Riddler</div></div></div>
<p>The Joker is considered one of the most-recognizable and iconic fictional characters in popular culture,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactIGN2_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactIGN2-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactComplex_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactComplex-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LubbockOnline_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LubbockOnline-183">[183]</a></sup> one of the best comic villains, and one of the greatest villains of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactLATimes_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactLATimes-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactIGNHero_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactIGNHero-185">[185]</a></sup> The character was well-liked following his debut, appearing in nine out of the first 12 <i>Batman</i> issues, and remained one of Batman's most popular foes throughout his publication.<sup id="cite_ref-CBR75Greatest_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBR75Greatest-186">[186]</a></sup> The character is considered one of the four top comic book characters, alongside Batman, Superman, and <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LubbockOnline_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LubbockOnline-183">[183]</a></sup> Indeed, when DC Comics released the original series of <i>Greatest Stories Ever Told</i> (1987–1988) featuring collections of stories about heroes like Batman and Superman, the Joker was the only villain included alongside them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XVIII_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XVIII-187">[187]</a></sup> The character has been the focus of ethical discussion on the desirability of Batman (who adheres to an unbreakable code forbidding killing) saving lives by murdering the Joker (a relentless dealer of death). These debates weigh the positive (stopping the Joker permanently) against its effect on Batman's character and the possibility that he might begin killing all criminals.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactMoral3_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactMoral3-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactMoral1_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactMoral1-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactMoral2_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactMoral2-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the Joker was number one on <i><a href="/wiki/Wizard_(magazine)" title="Wizard (magazine)">Wizard</a></i> magazine's "100 Greatest Villains of All Time."<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactWizard_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactWizard-190">[190]</a></sup> In 2008 <i>Wizard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s list of "200 Greatest Comic Book Characters of All Time" placed the Joker fifth,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactWizard2_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactWizard2-191">[191]</a></sup> and the character was eighth on <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Empire (magazine)">Empire</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s list of "50 Greatest Comic Book Characters" (the highest-ranked villain on both lists).<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactEmpire_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactEmpire-192">[192]</a></sup> In 2009, the Joker was second on <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a>'s list of "Top 100 Comic Book Villains,"<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactIGN_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactIGN-193">[193]</a></sup> and in 2011, <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i> named him "Comics' Greatest Supervillain."<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactWired_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactWired-194">[194]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/CollegeHumor" title="CollegeHumor">CollegeHumor</a>, and WhatCulture named the Joker the greatest comic book villain of all time<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactComplex_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactComplex-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactWhatCulture_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactWhatCulture-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCHumor2013_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCHumor2013-195">[195]</a></sup> while IGN listed him the top DC Comics villain in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactIGN2013_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactIGN2013-196">[196]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Newsarama" title="Newsarama">Newsarama</a> as the greatest Batman villain.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactRama2013_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactRama2013-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker's popularity (and his role as Batman's enemy) has involved the character in most <i>Batman</i>-related media, from television to video games.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactCBR_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactCBR-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Origin3_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin3-6">[6]</a></sup> These adaptations of the character have been received positively<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge2_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge2-19">[19]</a></sup> on film,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactAFI100_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactAFI100-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ImpactTime2013_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactTime2013-198">[198]</a></sup> television,<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactTVGuide_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactTVGuide-199">[199]</a></sup> and in video games.<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactGRadar_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactGRadar-200">[200]</a></sup> As in the comics, the character's personality and appearance shift; he is campy, ferocious or unstable, depending on the author and the intended audience.<sup id="cite_ref-GoldenAge2_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenAge2-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The character inspired theme-park roller coasters (<a href="/wiki/The_Joker%27s_Jinx" title="The Joker's Jinx">The Joker's Jinx</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CulturalCoaster2_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CulturalCoaster2-201">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CulturalCoaster1_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CulturalCoaster1-202">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Joker_(Six_Flags_Mexico)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Joker (Six Flags Mexico)">The Joker in Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Joker_(Six_Flags_Discovery_Kingdom)" title="The Joker (Six Flags Discovery Kingdom)">California</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-CulturalCoaster5_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CulturalCoaster5-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CulturalCoaster10_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CulturalCoaster10-204">[204]</a></sup> and The Joker Chaos Coaster),<sup id="cite_ref-CulturalCoaster50_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CulturalCoaster50-205">[205]</a></sup> and featured in story-based rides such as Justice League: Battle for Metropolis.<sup id="cite_ref-CulturalCoaster50_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CulturalCoaster50-205">[205]</a></sup> The Joker is one of the few comic book supervillains to be represented on children's merchandise and toys, appearing on items including action figures, trading cards, board games, money boxes, pajamas, socks, and shoes.<sup id="cite_ref-LubbockOnline_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LubbockOnline-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201519_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201519-206">[206]</a></sup> The Jokermobile was a popular toy; a Corgi <a href="/wiki/Die-cast_toy" title="Die-cast toy">die-cast metal</a> replica was successful during the 1950s, and in the 1970s a Joker-styled, <a href="/wiki/Flower_power" title="Flower power">flower power</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Type_2" title="Volkswagen Type 2">Volkswagen microbus</a> was manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Mego_Corporation" title="Mego Corporation">Mego</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201175_142-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201175-142">[142]</a></sup>  In 2015, <i>The Joker: A Serious Study of the Clown Prince of Crime</i> became the first academic book to be published about a supervillain.<sup id="cite_ref-LubbockOnline_183-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LubbockOnline-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 2012–2013, the Joker has inspired a large number of <a href="/wiki/Internet_meme" title="Internet meme">internet memes</a>, often focused on the character's portrayal in films (see below). According to Steven T. Wright of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Outline_(website)" title="The Outline (website)">The Outline</a></i>, the character "came to symbolize the archetype of the 'edgelord,' a vapid, self-styled provocateur who prides himself in his ability to '<a href="/wiki/Trigger_warning" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigger warning">trigger</a>' those who hold <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressive</a> viewpoints."<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> The phrase "We live in a society" is commonly associated with the Joker in memes; it garnered particular notoriety after a trailer for the film <i><a href="/wiki/Zack_Snyder%27s_Justice_League" title="Zack Snyder's Justice League">Zack Snyder's Justice League</a></i> (2021) featured Joker saying the line.<sup id="cite_ref-Society_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Society-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SocietyDeletedScene_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SocietyDeletedScene-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_other_media">In other media</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Joker_in_other_media" title="Joker in other media">Joker in other media</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:97px;max-width:97px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:152px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Smiling, middle-aged man in black jacket and shirt" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg/95px-Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg" decoding="async" width="95" height="152" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg/143px-Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg/190px-Mark_Hamill_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1113" data-file-height="1782" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:109px;max-width:109px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:152px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Heath_Ledger_(Berlin_Film_Festival_2006)_revised.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Young man with short beard, looking past the camera" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Heath_Ledger_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2006%29_revised.jpg/107px-Heath_Ledger_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2006%29_revised.jpg" decoding="async" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Heath_Ledger_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2006%29_revised.jpg/161px-Heath_Ledger_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2006%29_revised.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Heath_Ledger_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2006%29_revised.jpg/214px-Heath_Ledger_%28Berlin_Film_Festival_2006%29_revised.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1315" data-file-height="1885" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:130px;max-width:130px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:152px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Joaquin_Phoenix-1325.jpg_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Middle aged man with a short beard, looking past the camera" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Joaquin_Phoenix-1325.jpg_%28cropped%29.jpg/128px-Joaquin_Phoenix-1325.jpg_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="128" height="153" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Joaquin_Phoenix-1325.jpg_%28cropped%29.jpg/192px-Joaquin_Phoenix-1325.jpg_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Mark_Hamill" title="Mark Hamill">Mark Hamill</a> has voiced the Joker in animation and video games since 1992, and <a href="/wiki/Heath_Ledger" title="Heath Ledger">Heath Ledger</a> won a posthumous Academy Award for <a href="/wiki/Joker_(The_Dark_Knight)" title="Joker (The Dark Knight)">his interpretation of the character</a> in 2008's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">The Dark Knight</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Joaquin_Phoenix" title="Joaquin Phoenix">Joaquin Phoenix</a> won an Academy Award for his interpretation of the character in 2019's <i><a href="/wiki/Joker_(2019_film)" title="Joker (2019 film)">Joker</a></i>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>The Joker has appeared in a variety of media, including television series, animated and live-action films. <a href="/wiki/WorldCat" title="WorldCat">WorldCat</a> (a catalog of libraries in 170 countries) records over 250 productions featuring the Joker as a subject, including films, books, and video games,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201519_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee201519-206">[206]</a></sup> and Batman films featuring the character are typically the most successful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XXI_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinerPeaslee2015XXI-129">[129]</a></sup> The character's earliest on-screen adaptation was in the 1966 television series <i>Batman</i> and its film adaptation <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_(1966_film)" title="Batman (1966 film)">Batman</a></i>, in which he was played as a cackling prankster by Cesar Romero (reflecting his contemporary comic counterpart).<sup id="cite_ref-ImpactIGN2_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ImpactIGN2-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media4_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media4-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media66_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media66-211">[211]</a></sup> The Joker then appeared in the animated television series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Batman" title="The Adventures of Batman">The Adventures of Batman</a></i> (1968, voiced by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Storch" title="Larry Storch">Larry Storch</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-MediaStorch_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaStorch-212">[212]</a></sup> <i>The New Adventures of Batman</i> (1977, voiced by <a href="/wiki/Lennie_Weinrib" title="Lennie Weinrib">Lennie Weinrib</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-MediaWeinrib_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaWeinrib-213">[213]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Super_Powers_Team:_Galactic_Guardians" title="The Super Powers Team: Galactic Guardians">The Super Powers Team: Galactic Guardians</a></i> (1985, voiced by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Welker" title="Frank Welker">Frank Welker</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-MediaWelker_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaWelker-214">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaWelker2_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaWelker2-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>A darker version of the Joker (played by <a href="/wiki/Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson">Jack Nicholson</a>) made his film debut in 1989's <i>Batman</i>, which earned over $400 million at the worldwide box office. The role was a defining performance in Nicholson's career and was considered to overshadow Batman's, with film critic <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> saying that the audience must sometimes remind themselves not to root for the Joker.<sup id="cite_ref-Media3_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media3-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaEbert_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaEbert-217">[217]</a></sup> <i>Batman</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s success led to the 1992 television series, <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Animated_Series" title="Batman: The Animated Series">Batman: The Animated Series</a></i>. Voiced by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hamill" title="Mark Hamill">Mark Hamill</a>, the Joker retained the darker tone of the comics in stories acceptable for young children.<sup id="cite_ref-Media1_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media1-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media2_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media2-219">[219]</a></sup> Hamill's Joker is considered a defining portrayal, and he voiced the character in spin-off films (1993's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Mask_of_the_Phantasm" title="Batman: Mask of the Phantasm">Batman: Mask of the Phantasm</a></i> and 2000's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_Beyond:_Return_of_the_Joker" title="Batman Beyond: Return of the Joker">Batman Beyond: Return of the Joker</a></i>), video games (2001's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Vengeance" title="Batman: Vengeance">Batman: Vengeance</a></i>), related series (1996's <i><a href="/wiki/Superman:_The_Animated_Series" title="Superman: The Animated Series">Superman: The Animated Series</a></i>, 2000's <i><a href="/wiki/Static_Shock" title="Static Shock">Static Shock</a></i> and 2001's <i><a href="/wiki/Justice_League_(TV_series)" title="Justice League (TV series)">Justice League</a></i>), action figures, toys and amusement-park voiceovers.<sup id="cite_ref-Media8_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media8-220">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media9_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media9-221">[221]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaSShock_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaSShock-222">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning201117_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning201117-223">[223]</a></sup> A redesigned Joker, voiced by <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Michael_Richardson" title="Kevin Michael Richardson">Kevin Michael Richardson</a>, appeared in 2004's <i>The Batman</i>; Richardson was the first African-American to play the character.<sup id="cite_ref-MediaTheBatman_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTheBatman-224">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaTheBatman2_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTheBatman2-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Nolan" title="Christopher Nolan">Christopher Nolan</a>'s successful 2005 <i>Batman</i> film reboot, <i><a href="/wiki/Batman_Begins" title="Batman Begins">Batman Begins</a></i>, which ended with a teaser for the <a href="/wiki/The_Joker_(The_Dark_Knight)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Joker (The Dark Knight)">Joker</a>'s involvement in a sequel, the character appeared in 2008's <i>The Dark Knight</i>, played by <a href="/wiki/Heath_Ledger" title="Heath Ledger">Heath Ledger</a> as an avatar of anarchy and chaos.<sup id="cite_ref-Media5_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media5-226">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaTDK_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTDK-227">[227]</a></sup> While <i>Batman Begins</i> earned a worldwide total of $370 million;<sup id="cite_ref-MediaTDK4_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTDK4-228">[228]</a></sup> <i>The Dark Knight</i> earned over $1 billion and was the highest-grossing film of the year, setting several contemporary box-office records (including highest-grossing midnight opening, opening day and opening weekend).<sup id="cite_ref-MediaTDK2_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTDK2-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaTDK3_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTDK3-230">[230]</a></sup> Ledger won a posthumous <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Supporting_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Supporting Actor">Academy Award for Best Supporting Actor</a> for his performance, the first acting Oscar ever won for a superhero film.<sup id="cite_ref-Media6_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media6-231">[231]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media7_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media7-232">[232]</a></sup> The Joker has featured in a number of animated projects, such as 2009's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Brave_and_the_Bold" title="Batman: The Brave and the Bold">Batman: The Brave and the Bold</a></i> (voiced by <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bennett" title="Jeff Bennett">Jeff Bennett</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-MediaTBatB_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTBatB-233">[233]</a></sup> and 2011's <i><a href="/wiki/Young_Justice_(TV_series)" title="Young Justice (TV series)">Young Justice</a></i> (voiced by <a href="/wiki/Brent_Spiner" title="Brent Spiner">Brent Spiner</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-MediaYJ_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaYJ-234">[234]</a></sup> and comic book adaptations (including 2010's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Under_the_Red_Hood" title="Batman: Under the Red Hood">Batman: Under the Red Hood</a></i> and 2020's <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Death_in_the_Family" title="Batman: Death in the Family">Batman: Death in the Family</a></i>, in which he is voiced by <a href="/wiki/John_DiMaggio" title="John DiMaggio">John DiMaggio</a>).
</p><p>In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Emerson" title="Michael Emerson">Michael Emerson</a> voiced the character in a two-part animated adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Dark_Knight_Returns_(film)" title="Batman: The Dark Knight Returns (film)">The Dark Knight Returns</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Media12_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media12-235">[235]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media13_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media13-236">[236]</a></sup> The television series <i><a href="/wiki/Gotham_(TV_series)" title="Gotham (TV series)">Gotham</a></i> (2014–2019) explores the mythology of the Joker through twin brothers <a href="/wiki/Jerome_and_Jeremiah_Valeska" title="Jerome and Jeremiah Valeska">Jerome and Jeremiah Valeska</a> played by <a href="/wiki/Cameron_Monaghan" title="Cameron Monaghan">Cameron Monaghan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gotham1_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gotham1-237">[237]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jared_Leto" title="Jared Leto">Jared Leto</a> portrays the Joker in the <a href="/wiki/DC_Extended_Universe" title="DC Extended Universe">DC Extended Universe</a>, beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Suicide_Squad_(film)" title="Suicide Squad (film)">Suicide Squad</a></i> (2016);<sup id="cite_ref-MediaSuicide_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaSuicide-238">[238]</a></sup> Leto reprised the role in <i>Zack Snyder's Justice League</i> in 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zach_Galifianakis" title="Zach Galifianakis">Zach Galifianakis</a> voiced the character in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lego_Batman_Movie" title="The Lego Batman Movie">The Lego Batman Movie</a></i> (2017).<sup id="cite_ref-MediaTLBM_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaTLBM-240">[240]</a></sup> The 2019 film <i><a href="/wiki/Joker_(2019_film)" title="Joker (2019 film)">Joker</a></i> focuses on the origins of the Joker as portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Joaquin_Phoenix" title="Joaquin Phoenix">Joaquin Phoenix</a>. Although the film was controversial for its violence and portrayal of mental illness, Phoenix's performance received widespread acclaim.<sup id="cite_ref-WaPo:_Divisive_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo:_Divisive-241">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-THR:_GoldenLion_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THR:_GoldenLion-242">[242]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-THR:_Critics_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THR:_Critics-243">[243]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Joker2019BORecords_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joker2019BORecords-244">[244]</a></sup> Like <i>The Dark Knight</i> before it, <i>Joker</i> grossed over $1<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion at the box office, breaking contemporary financial records, and earned <a href="/wiki/List_of_accolades_received_by_Joker_(2019_film)" title="List of accolades received by Joker (2019 film)">numerous awards</a> including an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Academy Award for Best Actor</a> for Phoenix.<sup id="cite_ref-DeadlineJokerOscar_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeadlineJokerOscar-245">[245]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Joker2019BORecords_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joker2019BORecords-244">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Billion_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billion-246">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Joker has also been featured in video games. Hamill returned to voice the character in 2009's critically acclaimed <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Arkham_Asylum" title="Batman: Arkham Asylum">Batman: Arkham Asylum</a></i>, its equally praised 2011 sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Arkham_City" title="Batman: Arkham City">Batman: Arkham City</a></i> and the multiplayer <i><a href="/wiki/DC_Universe_Online" title="DC Universe Online">DC Universe Online</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-MediaDCU_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaDCU-247">[247]</a></sup> Hamill was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Troy_Baker" title="Troy Baker">Troy Baker</a> for the 2013 prequel, <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Arkham_Origins" title="Batman: Arkham Origins">Batman: Arkham Origins</a></i>, and the <i>Arkham</i> series' animated <span class="nowrap">spin-off</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Assault_on_Arkham" title="Batman: Assault on Arkham">Batman: Assault on Arkham</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Media8_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media8-220">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media10_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media10-248">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media11_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media11-249">[249]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AssaultonArkham_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AssaultonArkham-250">[250]</a></sup> while Hamill returned for the 2015 series finale, <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Arkham_Knight" title="Batman: Arkham Knight">Batman: Arkham Knight</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-GameArkKnight_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GameArkKnight-251">[251]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Epcar" title="Richard Epcar">Richard Epcar</a> has voiced the Joker in a series of fighting games including, <i><a href="/wiki/Mortal_Kombat_vs._DC_Universe" title="Mortal Kombat vs. DC Universe">Mortal Kombat vs. DC Universe</a></i> (2008),<sup id="cite_ref-MediaMKvsDCU_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaMKvsDCU-252">[252]</a></sup>  <i><a href="/wiki/Injustice:_Gods_Among_Us" title="Injustice: Gods Among Us">Injustice: Gods Among Us</a></i> (2013),<sup id="cite_ref-MediaIGAU_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaIGAU-253">[253]</a></sup> its sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Injustice_2" title="Injustice 2">Injustice 2</a></i> (2017),<sup id="cite_ref-MediaIJ2_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaIJ2-254">[254]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mortal_Kombat_11" title="Mortal Kombat 11">Mortal Kombat 11</a></i> (2019).<sup id="cite_ref-JokerMortalKombat11_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JokerMortalKombat11-255">[255]</a></sup> The character also appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/Lego_Batman:_The_Videogame" title="Lego Batman: The Videogame">Lego Batman: The Videogame</a></i> (2008), <i><a href="/wiki/Lego_Batman_2:_DC_Super_Heroes" title="Lego Batman 2: DC Super Heroes">Lego Batman 2: DC Super Heroes</a></i> (2012) and its <a href="/wiki/Lego_Batman:_The_Movie_%E2%80%93_DC_Super_Heroes_Unite" title="Lego Batman: The Movie – DC Super Heroes Unite">animated adaptation</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Lego_Batman_3:_Beyond_Gotham" title="Lego Batman 3: Beyond Gotham">Lego Batman 3: Beyond Gotham</a></i> (2014) (the latter three voiced by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Corey_Smith" title="Christopher Corey Smith">Christopher Corey Smith</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-MediaLego1_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaLego1-256">[256]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaLego2_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaLego2-257">[257]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MediaLego3_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MediaLego3-258">[258]</a></sup> as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Lego_DC_Super-Villains" title="Lego DC Super-Villains">Lego DC Super-Villains</a></i> (2018), with the role reprised by Hamill. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ingruber" title="Anthony Ingruber">Anthony Ingruber</a> voices the Joker in <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Telltale_Series" title="Batman: The Telltale Series">Batman: The Telltale Series</a></i> (2016)<sup id="cite_ref-GameTelltale_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GameTelltale-259">[259]</a></sup> and its sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_The_Enemy_Within" title="Batman: The Enemy Within">Batman: The Enemy Within</a></i> (2017).<sup id="cite_ref-GameTelltale2_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GameTelltale2-260">[260]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-BronzeAge4-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BronzeAge4_44-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080411230610/http://www.scifi.com/scifiwire/index.php?category=5&id=40748">"Batman Artist Rogers is Dead"</a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.scifi.com/scifiwire/index.php?category=5&id=40748">the original</a> on April 11, 2008<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">May 2,</span> 2008</span>. <q>Even though their Batman run was only six issues, the three laid the foundation for later Batman comics. Their stories include the classic 'Laughing Fish' (in which the Joker's face appeared on fish); they were adapted for <i>Batman: The Animated Series</i> in the 1990s. Earlier drafts of the 1989 Batman film with Michael Keaton as the Dark Knight were based heavily on their work</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&rft.genre=unknown&rft.btitle=Batman+Artist+Rogers+is+Dead&rft.pub=Sci+Fi&rft.date=2007-03-28&rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.scifi.com%2Fscifiwire%2Findex.php%3Fcategory%3D5%26id%3D40748&rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJoker+%28character%29" class="Z3988"></span></span>
